## 2390

By the Grace of Hashem
6 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn

To the esteemed *avreich*, G-d fearing, learned and pious, etc., R’ Yisrael, may he live.

Shalom u’vracha!

After the long hiatus, I was pleased to receive your letter from Erev Shabbos Parshas Shemos. Regarding your justification for not writing sooner—that you had nothing important to write about—the wonder at this is even greater. For according to the language of the *Zohar* (end of Part I), every single day must be utilized in fulfilling the mission of the Ribono shel Olam to illuminate one’s portion in the world—namely, to exert influence upon oneself and also upon one’s surroundings. Surely, during the months that we have not been in contact, you could have, and should have, been doing this, and consequently, there would have been room for many letters.

However, one does not cry over the past. I write this only as a catalyst for the future, that all of the above should be fulfilled—and not merely to the extent of one’s needs, but in abundance, as the *Midrash Esther Rabbah* (at the end) says: "Peace in abundance, blessing in abundance," etc.

The main point is that since Hashem has granted you the merit of knowing the teachings of Chassidus, its guidance, and its customs, you can be the conduit to transmit these insights to our brethren, the Children of Israel, who currently require them in the Italian language. You should be working on this with the greatest energy—namely, translating into Italian accessible topics from the *sichos* and even from the *maamarim*, especially before the *moadim*, *yomei depagra*, the *yom hahilula*, etc. As I have written to you previously, the esteemed Rabbi and Chassid, G-d fearing, learned and pious, engaged in *tzedakah*, R’ Mordechai Perlow, may he live, will surely assist you in this with all his ability. It is well known that our *Rebbe* the Alter Rebbe promised that those who follow in his footsteps succeed when they act in matters of Torah and *mitzvos*. It is superfluous to repeat once again that if this involves any expenses in the usual manner, I will cover them.

I await good news regarding this as soon as possible, for this is a matter akin to *Talmud Torah* in its entirety; when one delays for even a moment, it is a deficiency that cannot be filled, since every part of time is obligated in *Talmud Torah* in its own right. Further elaboration is unnecessary.

Regarding what is unclear to you in *Rambam*, *Hilchos Melachim*, concerning the authority of the King and the power of his rule, and the difficulty you see in reconciling the law that the King has the right to impose taxes on the people with the law that he is forbidden to accumulate wealth for himself—I do not see any contradiction here.

For in general, according to the law of our Torah, there are many matters that require a *Beis Din* in order for the public to be obligated to obey them. This is the case with imposing taxes and levies upon the people. The power of a King is that he does not need a *Beis Din* for this; rather, he himself can act in this regard, just as in other matters where the King has the authority to act on his own. These are detailed in *Rambam*, *Hilchos Melachim*, and in other places. It is self-understood that the tax enters the possession of the King, and it is in his hands to distribute it as he sees fit. However, just like any other Jew—and even more so, given the magnitude of the responsibility upon him—he must conduct the affairs of his office faithfully. As the *Rambam* puts it, the King’s heart is the heart of the entire congregation. He must distribute the funds according to the requirements of the state of the monarchy, for the army, his servants, etc., and also for himself and his affairs, in such a way that nothing is done in vain. For even regarding a single idle horse, one transgresses the will of Hashem. This means that a King of Israel, according to the laws of the Torah, possesses the greatest authority, yet the Torah obligates him to be more humble than any other person and not to accumulate for himself any matters that are not necessary for the essential awe required for the peace of the state, etc.

See also the *sefer Derech Mitzvosecha* by the *Admur* the Tzemach Tzedek, *Mitzvas Minui Melech* (page 108a and onwards), where he writes: "The root of the matter intended in the appointment of a King is that through him and by his hand, the Jewish people will be nullified (*batelim*) to Hashem." See his words there, where he explains this at length.

Enclosed is a copy of the *maamar* that was delivered on the *yom hahilula* of my father-in-law, the *Rebbe*, of blessed memory, last year, in which you will surely find much substance.

Regarding the authority of the King—see the *sefer Toras Neviim* by the Maharatz Chayes.

## 2,391

B"H, 6 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi, the G-d-fearing, Torah-learned, and diligent communal worker, R' Hillel, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

...Thank you for your comments regarding my letter from this past 19 Kislev, and regarding what you wrote concerning the passage "known" (*yedu'im*): You suggest citing the Zohar, Parshas Balak—presumably page 196b. However, there is no proof there that it refers to the neshamah before its descent below; on the contrary. Consequently, it does not pertain to the matter discussed in my letter, which specifically concerns the state prior to the descent into the body, as is understood.

Regarding the passage "although" (*af al pi*), where you noted that "a reliable source for this is in Tanna D'bei Eliyahu on the verse 'Blessed is the man'":

Perhaps you are referring to Tanna D'bei Eliyahu Rabbah, beginning of Chapter 18 (it is surprising that you did not cite the chapter, page, and section of the book; after the statement of Rav Ashi regarding the forgetfulness of our generations (Eruvin 53a), what can we say in their wake?). Seemingly, it is not relevant to the case at hand in my letter, as that source discusses a person’s needs, such as eating and drinking, etc., or perhaps needs in the World to Come—namely, the reward for mitzvos...

With blessings for success in communal matters and in your personal affairs, awaiting good news.

## 2,792

B"H, 6 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

To the G-d-fearing, esteemed, and distinguished, who engages in communal needs, R' Dovid, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

You already know my opinion, that in the current time (and perhaps at all times), one must not wait until the other person comes to ask for Yiddishkeit. Rather, one must go to them and awaken their appetite for Yiddishkeit. By way of a witty interpretation, one can say regarding this, as it is written, "And you shall walk in His ways; just as the Holy One, Blessed be He, etc." — the Ribbono shel Olam also came first, before everything, to Har Sinai, and waited for the Yidden, who were still sleeping at that time, as is recounted in the Midrashim of our Sages.

And when one thinks properly into this statement of our Sages, "The poor person does more for the householder than the householder does for the poor person"—which is true both in gashmius and in ruchnius—one has surely never seen a businessman sit in his room and wait until someone comes to him and says, "You need a livelihood, so I can help you with that." The elaboration on this is superfluous.

Enclosed is the maamar that was said last year on the yom hahilula of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory. I am certain that you will act with a generous eye to share it and bring merit to others as well.

With blessings.

#### ***2,792***

**R' Dovid:** Tannenhaus.

## 2393

B"H, 6 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, learned, and distinguished man of many accomplishments, a man of knowledge, scion of a noble lineage, etc., R' Gad, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

I have heard much about you and your activities in general, and specifically regarding your interest and participation in the life of Anash, may they live, in the Holy Land, may it be rebuilt and established. This is in addition to what I heard about you from my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory. Your letter of 10 Teves has reached me, and it brought me great joy. I hope that this will be a good beginning for a correspondence between us that will lead to an increase in the activities of each of us. It is a well-known saying of the Mitteler Rebbe that when two Jews come into contact, there are two divine souls against one animal soul...

It was with special satisfaction that I read at the end of your letter that you are planning to publish your memoirs. One part will discuss the settlement during the final period of Turkish rule, and the second part will cover your years of service as a judge during the thirty years of the British Mandate, and it also describes your meeting with my father-in-law, the Rebbe. A few days after your letter arrived, the chapters themselves also reached me, including the chapter describing your visit inside the Me'aras HaMachpeilah. I read it all with interest, and it was pleasant for me to see, both in the lines and between the lines, your warm feelings in describing the memories of days gone by, and especially your description of the Jerusalem atmosphere and the Chassidic way of life in general, in connection with the Yomim Tovim and significant events, etc.

If at all times and in all places it is appropriate to publicize matters such as these, then all the more so in these days of ours and in our Holy Land, may it be redeemed by Moshiach Tzidkeinu. To our sorrow, many of the younger generation negate our glorious past, and there are even some who know nothing of the past at all and wish to calculate our history starting only from the last few years or a few years prior. From such a clouded perspective, they arrive at false conclusions, etc. Seemingly, for one who is on the scene, elaboration on this is surely superfluous.

May your strength be bolstered to instill, through your aforementioned memoirs and your activities in general, a love for the tradition of our fathers within the circles of our people, and for everything holy to our holy ancestors who sacrificed their lives for it. They knew with certainty that our tradition and its values are the eternal values of the entire Jewish people, and it is the point of its existence throughout all the days of the galus, until we merit the true and complete Geulah.

With appreciation and blessing for long days and good years, fruitful in good deeds.

[The Rebbe]

I am not mentioning whether to return the copy of your notes to you; they will remain with me until I receive your instructions regarding this.

I would be interested to know if you happen to know the details of the visit of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, to the Me'aras HaMachpeilah, and how far inside he entered. Thank you in advance.

## 2394

B”H, 7 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, and learned, whose work is the work of Heaven, R’ Shlomo Chaim, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your two letters from 19 Teves and Erev Rosh Chodesh Shevat, along with the enclosures. Surely, in the meantime, you have received my detailed letter regarding the administration of Toras Emes in Lod, as well as the encouragement to organize the Yahrtzeit of 10 Shevat, the coming day, in an appropriate manner. Since, according to the reports received here, the outreach to students of other yeshivos at the 19 Kislev farbrengen made a strong impression, the past should teach us for the future to act in this way as well. Even though this means that part of the farbrengen must be conducted in different terms—suitable for those who are beginners in this matter—I rely on your insight.

2) I have already written many times, and I will repeat it now, that there is no *kapida* on my part, etc. Regarding what you wrote about studying the Torah of the *Nasi* of the generation—my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, *ztzukl”h, nbg”m, zy”a*—I did not come, G-d forbid, to negate the study of the *maamarim* of Chassidus of the [previous] *Nesi’im*, who are his predecessors; that is obvious. Rather, every student must stand in a state of *hiskashrus* appropriate to his time and generation, and *hiskashrus* is through the Torah of the *Nasi*, as explained in several letters and also transcribed in the *Hayom Yom*. Therefore, I expressed my opinion that such study is specifically necessary. Since, according to your letter, this matter has already been arranged for some time, there is no room for any concern regarding *kapida*.

3) My letter to [---], may he live, was surely received in due time. He will certainly add an explanation to the aforementioned that one must be extremely careful regarding physical health, as the Rambam writes in *Hilchos Deios*, for otherwise it disrupts the service of Hashem, as is also clearly evident in actual experience and in the aforementioned matter itself. And even if, for the sake of maintaining health, he is forced to arrange his study and prayer in a way different from what he is accustomed to—and perhaps for him this might feel like a "nullification of your Torah," G-d forbid—he must find for himself the appropriate way to explain this, especially according to the saying of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe Rashab, *n”e*, that it is possible for the *Yetzer Hara* to dress itself in a "silk *kapata*."

4) All of the above is also an instruction for conduct with the other students: in this generation, extra caution is needed regarding their health. Regarding this generation, one can apply—and with greater emphasis—what our *Rabbeinu HaZaken* wrote in *Iggeres HaTeshuvah*, Chapter 3: indeed, all of this applies to a strong and healthy person, etc., but [for] one who [is not]... and the same applies to fasts and self-afflictions of a certain measure, even of the type discussed in *Tanya*, Chapter 27, and delaying one’s meal, etc. Especially since our *Nasi* has already instructed us in the path of *avodah* for our generation as well, as in the statement of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe Maharash (transcribed in his *Sefer HaToldos*, page 72, at the end)...

With blessings for success in your holy work, and regards to all those who are connected to you and influenced by you in particular, awaiting good news.

## 2397

B"H, 8 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn

To the illustrious Rabbi and Tzaddik, scion of holy ancestors, noble and renowned, etc.
Rabbi Shalom Yechezkel Shraga, may he live and be well.

Shalom u’Bracha!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter from the 4th of Parshas Bo, in which you write regarding the question of using medicinal substances to turn white hair black or yellow, and whether this constitutes a violation of the prohibition, "A man shall not wear a woman's garment" (*lo yilbash*).

Time does not permit an exhaustive discussion, and you have already noted in your letter that this is similar to the law regarding drinking a *kos ikarin* (a sterilization potion) (*Shabbos 110b*), which most authorities hold is prohibited by Torah law (the various opinions are cited in *Otzar HaPoskim* to *Even HaEzer* 6, subsection 70).

One could argue that, on the contrary, regarding castration, the Sages derived from the verse, "You shall not do [so]"—that it is prohibited to perform the act (*Shabbos* ibid.; or they derived it from the word *bachem*—see *HaEmek She'eilah*, end of section 105). This is not the case with *lo yilbash*, where we do not find such a derivation, and we should not derive new prohibitions on our own if the act does not involve direct contact with the hair.

If so, according to the *Meiri* (*Shabbos* ibid.) and others who hold that the aforementioned derivation is merely an *asmachta* (a scriptural support for a Rabbinic law), yet they still rule that drinking a *kos ikarin* is prohibited by Rabbinic law, then in the present case, too, it would be prohibited by Rabbinic law.

One should not raise a difficulty from the case in *Nazir* (59a), where it is permitted *ab initio* because it does not involve touching the hair; for the hair of the armpit and the pubic area is different, as it is lenient because the act is not noticeable (*see Responsa Tzemach Tzedek, Yoreh Deah 93*).

Alternatively, one could argue that in the present case it is entirely permitted, since we do not find in the *Gemara* that it is prohibited, and we should not invent an *asmachta* on our own.

However, all this holds true according to Rashi in *Nazir* (ibid.), who says it is permitted **because** it does not involve touching the hair. But according to the *Rosh* (ibid.), the permission is because, through his clothing, it does not constitute "beautifying like a woman." Therefore, in the present case, which is indeed a "beautifying like a woman"—as he is taking the aforementioned substances to beautify himself—it falls under the prohibition of *lo yilbash*.

Since this is a matter of logical reasoning, and furthermore, it is a *kal v’chomer* (a fortiori argument) from the prohibition against looking in a mirror (*Yoreh Deah* 156), one could perhaps interpret Rashi’s language this way as well, even though it is somewhat forced.

It is worth noting: a) There are those who distinguish between black hair and red hair, as explained in the *Acharonim* cited in *Sdei Chemed*, *Klallim Lamed*, subsection 15. b) Regarding whether dyeing white hair black is a Torah or Rabbinic prohibition—in *Responsa Shoel U’Meishiv* (Kama, Part 1), it is held to be Rabbinic, but most *Acharonim* disagree; see *Responsa Minchas Elazar*, Part 4, and the books mentioned in the annotations of Rabbi Baron to the *Kitzur Shulchan Aruch*, subsection 71.

c) Since the aforementioned substances are used **with the intent** to beautify the hair, the entire debate regarding an unintentional act (*pesik reisha*) is not applicable here (see the extensive discussion in *HaEmek She'eilah* ibid., and the opinions in *Sdei Chemed* ibid.).

Consequently, even if their effect is not entirely certain, it is prohibited to use them.

Enclosed is a copy of the *Maamar* that was just published, which was delivered on the *Yahrtzeit* last year, which I am certain will interest you.

With respect and blessing,

[Signature]

## 2398

B"H, 8 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, and refined, involved in communal needs,
R' Dovid Nosson, may he live and be well,

Greetings and blessings!

I received your letter with the good news in due time, and I hereby reiterate my blessing upon the birth of your grandson, the son of your son, R' Moshe Shimshon, may he live. May the parents, may they live, derive from him much Chassidishe nachas, and may you and your wife, may you live, derive from him and from all your offspring, may they live, much nachas and delight for long, good days and years.

Regarding your question as to the location of the Rambam’s responsum concerning the sanctity of the Ashuri script: it is printed in various places, including the edition of the Rambam printed in the United States by the Shulsinger brothers. As for the substance of the matter—teaching the form of the letters of the Ashuri script to young children—if the intention in those sources were to teach the Ashuri script, surely this would have been practiced throughout the Diaspora, yet such a thing has never been heard of.

Enclosed is the maamar delivered last year on the yahrtzeit of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory. I am certain you will act with a generous eye to share the merit with the public.

With blessings.

## 2399

B"H, 9 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

To the Agudas N'shei U’Bnos Chabad in the city of Cleveland,
May they live.

Blessings and peace!

It was with satisfaction that I received the news that at the beginning of the week, an Agudas N'shei U’Bnos Chabad was organized in Cleveland. May Hashem Yisbarach help—and He will certainly help—that through the appropriate work of the members and the leaders, this will be successful. Success in your own personal avodah and that of your families, in all that is necessary according to the directives of our Nesi’im, the Rebbes, and in our generation, the Nasi HaDor, my father-in-law, the Rebbe, whose Yom Hillula is tomorrow—may his merit protect us. And may you also succeed in expanding your circle by drawing in more women, daughters of Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov.

It is well known what our Rebbe, the Alter Rebbe, said: that Chassidus is not something for a specific group among Jews, but rather that Chassidus belongs to the entire Klal Yisrael. Every man and woman who works to realize this desire, according to the words of the Alter Rebbe, is promised that the Rebbes will draw down success in this work.

It goes without saying that when one is successful in *avodah ruchnis*—in spiritual work—it naturally brings blessing and success in *inyanim gashmiyim*—material matters—regarding you and your families, may they live. May Hashem grant you success.

With blessings to each and every one of you, and to all your household members, may they live, with all that they need, both materially and spiritually.

## 2,400

B"H, 9 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

I acknowledge receipt of your letter from Motzaei Shabbos Parshas Va'era, regarding your inquiry about the path of avodah against the schemes of the yetzer hara. Our great Nasi, our Rabbeinu HaZaken, has already instructed us in Tanya, Chapter 29: "And also thunder against it with a voice of noise and rage to humble it... and you are a scoundrel... denying the truth which is visible to the eyes..." The reason for this is that, in truth, there is no substance at all to the sitra achra; it exists only so that a person will be aroused to overcome it and humble it, and then it is automatically nullified and pushed away. However, one must be careful to avoid what our Sages said, that "whoever flatters the wicked will eventually..." Heaven forbid. For this reason, the aforementioned conduct of strength and overcoming is required as it should be. This applies as well to the agents of the yetzer hara, etc. Further elaboration on this is superfluous, especially in these days preceding the Hillula, which is a day of segulah for all who follow in the footsteps of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, holding fast to his ways and conduct, and strengthening the institutions he founded and led—in which there must be "ascending in holiness" with expansion and growth. It is beyond any doubt that they will have the upper hand, as per the saying of the Mitteler Rebbe (transcribed in Sefer Kitzurim LeTanya, end of page 122) that it was decreed in the Heavenly Beis Din that in every matter of Torah, yiras shamayim, and good character traits, the hand of those who are connected to him and follow in his footsteps will be on the upper hand. May Hashem grant them merit and success to see this tangibly, and also with physical eyes, as soon as possible.

2) Everything stated above is written not only for you, may you live and be well, but also for your wife and relatives, may they live and be well, since it appears from your letter that they are lacking in the matter of "his heart was lifted up in the ways of Hashem."

3) Enclosed is the maamar that was said last year on the day of the Hillula, the 10th of Shevat. Surely you will act with a "good eye" to share it for the benefit of the many.

With blessings for true health in physicality and true health in spirituality for each and every one of them, awaiting good tidings.

## 2411

B"H, 9 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing and refined, occupied with communal needs,
Rabbi Ben Zion, may he live, Shaliach

Peace and blessing!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letters from 26 Teves and 4 Shevat. It is surprising that, judging by the spirit of your letter, you have become disheartened by those who cast an envious eye upon the activities of Lubavitch and its leadership. After all, such things have existed since the days of Moshe Rabbeinu, whom people criticized, etc. Yet, the one who walks in integrity to fulfill his role and mission to illuminate his surroundings will, in the end, be victorious. As explained in the sicha of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, "Before the truth, everything becomes null." As for the time it takes until they are nullified, in any event, we must thank Hashem that the concealments and hiddenness have weakened, and consequently, the disturbances and battles against Chabad have also weakened. As for what is in their hearts, or even what they say, let them have what is theirs; we are "workers of the day," and as the Tzemach Tzedek said, "We make it bright," and therefore there is no time for battles, for one must rebuild what is ruined...

With blessings for success in your holy work, in good health for you, your wife, and all your household, may they live.

## 2003

B.H., 10 Shevat, the Hilula of my sainted father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory.
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To all those gathered and participating in the fourteenth annual United States celebration of the Merkaz Yeshivos Tomchei Temimim Lubavitch, may Hashem grant them life.

Peace and Blessing!

Today’s annual celebration of the Merkaz Yeshivos Tomchei Temimim Lubavitch takes place a few days after the yahrtzeit of my sainted father-in-law, the Rebbe, the founder of the Lubavitcher yeshivos in America. It is held under the impression of the spiritual hisorerus that these days bring to all who have the privilege of being connected to the great and eternal activities of this tzaddik and leader of the generation, may his merit protect us.

On this day of the Hilula of my sainted father-in-law, the Rebbe, I send this greeting, call to action, and blessing to all those gathered and participating in the annual celebration of the Lubavitcher yeshivos.

The words and actions of a tzaddik are eternal.

The Lubavitcher yeshivos are a part of the eternal and great inheritance that the Baal Hilula has left us—left us to guard, to increase, and to expand.

Like every living thing, whose vitality is seen in growth and development, and like every דבר שבקדושה, which we must always elevate in holiness, the Lubavitcher yeshivos live and grow with the help of Hashem Yisbarach. They spread Torah and fear of Heaven, and they educate a generation that is permeated with mesiras nefesh for Torah and the performance of mitzvos in practice—not only in their own lives, but also in the entirety of Jewish life in America and everywhere else.

The yeshivos must continue to receive every opportunity to grow and expand.

Therefore, I turn to you all with an urgent and heartfelt request: to strengthen yourselves and to strengthen one another, to give and to provide maximum assistance to the Lubavitcher yeshivos, so that they may continue their holy work without hindrances or obstacles caused by the heavy financial burden, and with the fullest capacity to grow and ascend in both quantity and quality.

During his lifetime in this world, my sainted father-in-law, the Rebbe, would bestow blessing and success upon all who turned to him, and especially upon those who took part in his holy work.

Even now, he stands on high, serving and awakening great mercy to bestow blessing and success upon all who keep open the channels to receive his blessings—in general, and in particular, through participation in his mosdos and by following in his ways.

And surely he will continue to draw down and bring to the participants, their households, and their families, may they live, blessing and success in everything each one needs—children, life, and abundant sustenance.

With respect and great blessing.

## 2404

2404

B"H, 11 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn

To the congregants of the Nusach HaAri Beis Knesses of the Anash of Lubavitch in Dorchester, led by the Chairman, R' Shraga Asher Fein, and the Gabbaim, may they live:

Shalom and Bracha!

I was pleased to receive greetings from you through the esteemed Chossid, the G-d-fearing man, who occupies himself with communal needs, R' Yehuda Leib Halevi Horowitz, along with the enclosed check for $200 from this past Yom Kippur appeal.

Since every living thing grows—especially a matter of holiness—I was pleased that this year’s appeal proceeds were greater than last year’s, although it is still far from sufficient regarding the needs and expenses of the matters for which this money is used. I hope, however, that this will be a good beginning to continue to add more and more.

It is well known, the saying of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, whose Hillula we observed yesterday: when a Yid resolves to give a larger sum to tzedakah, even if at that moment he does not see the possibility of doing so or where he will take the money from, new channels are immediately opened for him Above, so that he will have an *osafas bracha* (an increase in blessing) in his parnassah, and he will be able to fulfill his good promise in actual practice.

And to these words of the Rebbe, one may add: since tzedakah is a portion of a maaser or a chomesh, a decree is made Above for an increase of at least five times what he decided to give to tzedakah, and the remaining four parts remain with him for his own personal matters.

With the blessing that this mitzvah of tzedakah should bring to each of you the blessings of Hashem Yisbarach—which are entirely tzedakah—and that this should be expressed in whatever each one of you requires.

## B'teh

B'teh

11 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, and distinguished individual, occupied with communal needs,
Rabbi Yehuda Leib Halevi

Peace and blessing!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter from Parshas Va'era, in which you begin by writing, "We have no news."

This opening immediately gives rise to wonder, for "He looked into the Torah and created the **world**." Since the Torah must be "as new" to us every single day, it is all the more certain that every person—who is called a "small world"—must renew himself every day. This is especially true regarding the fulfillment of the mission placed upon him by Divine Providence. For one such as yourself, who merited to study at Yeshivas Tomchei Temimim in Lubavitch and "saw the lights," there is surely no need to elaborate on this.

It is understood that my intention is simple: that everyone must be active in their city. There is no limit to goodness, and there is always room to act. As for the lack of action—even though in matters of teshuvah it is obvious that there is a distinction compared to acting improperly—regarding the essential purpose of man’s creation, which is, as explained in Tanya, "to make for Him, may He be blessed, a dwelling place in the lower worlds," even a lack of action is akin to a breach of trust in one's mission. This is because hours are passing that were given to him for the aforementioned purpose. As is well known, the interpretation of the verse, "Days were fashioned, and for Him one of them" [is that] he does not utilize them.

It is also understood that I do not intend to offer mussar, Heaven forbid, but rather, according to the words of our Sages, "one only urges those who are already diligent." This is also based on the promise according to the ruling of our Rebbes, that whoever wishes to act as their shaliach, it is a ruling of the Heavenly Beis Din that in every matter of Torah, yiras shamayim, and good character traits, the hand of those who are connected to them and walk in their footsteps will be on top (see Sefer Kitzurei Tanya, page 122).

Enclosed is the maamar that was said last year on the yahrtzeit of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory. I am certain you will act with a "good eye" to share it and bring merit to the many.

Also enclosed are my letters and receipts for the Nusach Ari synagogue of the Anash of Lubavitch. I am certain that if any explanation is needed, you will find the appropriate words for it.

With blessing and thanks in advance for the above.

***

**Rabbi Yehuda Leib:** Horowitz.

**Ruling of the Heavenly Beis Din:** See above, Letter 2395, and the references in the footnotes there.

## B'Tav

B'Tav

B"H, 11 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

I have seen your letter to the secretariat in which you ask about the reason for the lack of letters. I have already written many times that there is no issue of *kapida* or the like here; rather, due to the multitude of preoccupations and the many responses required for people, a long time passes until the turn for every letter arrives. Furthermore, there is priority given to responding to letters that are practically relevant, especially those from individuals who are not yet within the *daled amos* of Chassidei Chabad, as responding to them is also a matter of drawing them toward the study of the teachings of Chassidus and to follow its customs and guidance. Consequently, it is understood that one should not take the lack of letters from me into account.

However, since it is important to know about everything that is happening in our world, and in particular, the letters from you should be constant and regular. There is benefit in this, both for the matters about which you write and often for other matters as well, for after all, it is known to all that all matters of Chabad are connected and bound to one another. The success and growth of one of them makes an impression both internally and externally. This also explains my letters...

Which I am certain have already been received, in which I expressed my surprise at the lack of letters from you, etc.

The *maamar* for the *yom hilula* of last year, which was sent to you, may you live... has surely been received by now, and may it be the will of Hashem that it be of benefit to the speaker, the listener, and also to those who study it afterward.

## 2,707

Brooklyn.

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter dated 3 Shevat. When I visit the holy resting place of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, I will mention you for a speedy recovery.

You conclude your letter by responding to my request that you inform me of the nature of the people in your surroundings and that you influence them. You note that there are about fifty people there, but most are not yet observant of Torah and mitzvos, etc., and that their talk is surely at the expense of the suffering you are enduring.

I am surprised by your conclusion, especially since I wrote clearly—as you also quote in your letter—that the purpose of knowing about them and their nature is so that you might influence them. Since you yourself write that they are embarrassed before you regarding certain matters, this is absolute proof that you must find the approach to engage them in conversation. Consequently, you should utilize the time you are still with them to speak to them, moving from the easier matters to the more difficult ones.

May it be the will of Hashem that He grant you success in fulfilling what has been said, "little by little I will drive them out"—to drive out the evil that exists in your surroundings. Since you were brought there, you have surely been given the strength for this avodah. Based on what is brought in several seforim, it is almost certain that this is also the channel for your physical healing. May Hashem grant you success.

With blessings as stated above.

## 2111

B"H, 14 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, and distinguished individual, occupied with communal needs,
Rabbi Yitzchak Zalman, may he live and be well,

Greetings and blessings!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your Pidyon Nefesh... May Hashem grant you success, so that from this small seed, great and wondrous growths will sprout.

Regarding what you wrote, "I am small," my intent is not regarding quantity. For we see tangibly, even in the physical world, that quantity is not as significant as quality. This is one of the lessons from recent discoveries regarding the atom—that a single grain can destroy an entire settlement. And since the measure of good is greater than the measure of [punishment], how much more so is this true regarding the power of a human being hidden within the physicality of a simple person. It is incomparable, like the value of the finite compared to the infinite, which is hidden in a single grain of spirituality—and who can say, in a single grain of Elokus that is found within every son and daughter of Yisrael.

However, just as in the aforementioned example, a jolt—*a treisel*—is needed to reveal the hidden powers. So too, regarding spirituality, and even Elokus, which is found in a state of concealment and hiddenness in the world so that one will search for it. As is known, regarding the interpretation of the verse, "A wise son makes a father glad"—the father hides from the son so that he will search for him. It is also known what is told about one of the great figures of Yisrael, who saw his grandson playing hide-and-seek with children his age. When the grandson hid and the other child went about his business, the grandson began to cry because he had hidden and no one was searching for him. And so it is, to make an infinite distinction, in the analogy, of course.

The responsibility of each one of us in this generation is even greater, since one no longer needs to search, but only to publicize what is already present, at which point one sees Elokus tangibly. This is especially true for the students of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, who walk in his ways in their daily lives, in thought, speech, and actual deed.

I await good news regarding your good activities—and not only regarding matters that one might expect from *olam'she* (worldly) people, but also in that which my father-in-law, the Rebbe, hoped to see in each one of his students.

## 212

B"H, 14 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

Through the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing and learned, involved in communal needs, etc., [Name], your request was delivered to me to arouse mercy regarding the son of your brother, may he live, who is currently at odds with his father, may he live. When I am at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, I will mention them, that peace may dwell between them and that Hashem may lead them in a path of peace and success in all that they require.

Now, according to what is explained in the seforim, when there is no peace between two people, the cause is not always what is stated openly or even what comes to their own minds. It is possible that there is a deeper, internal cause of which even the nefesh habahamis is unaware. Therefore, it is necessary to clarify if the situation is such that his brother, may he live, exerted himself in the upbringing of the aforementioned son to ensure he received a Jewish education to the extent that it was within his power, and whether he was born in purity.

May Hashem grant him the merit to share good news regarding the improvement of his son’s health, may he live, in both physical and spiritual matters, and likewise regarding his brother and his brother’s son, may they live.

With blessings.

## B'Tid

By the Grace of Hashem, 17 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

To my esteemed friend, the brilliant Torah scholar, the G-d-fearing, the scion of holy lineage, a branch of the noble cedar, my friend, the Rav Yehuda Aryeh, shlita.

Peace and blessing!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter dated Wednesday. I also received the two volumes of the new *Lev Aryeh*, Part I and Part II. Many thanks for this precious gift. As a token of appreciation, and in gratitude for the gift—relying on the words of our Sages (Bava Metzia 84a), "I thought I would say something, and he would ask me twenty-four questions... do I not know that I spoke well?"—I am taking the liberty, in haste, of including a few notes at the end of my letter.

In recent weeks, the *Tanya* (referred to in the approbation of the Rabbis of Krakow as *Sefer Tanya Kadisha*) was printed—for the first time in the United States, and indeed in the entire Southern Hemisphere—and I have instructed that it be sent to you, shlita, together with the *Kuntres* in which the Rebbe, my father-in-law, of blessed memory, explains the statement of our Rebbe, the author of the *Tanya*, that in the Southern Hemisphere there was no *Matan Torah*. I trust that through those who carry out his will, you will confirm their receipt.

With esteem, honor, and blessing,
Your friend.

***

P.S.

Regarding the beginning of Part I, page 1: The precision regarding why it is sometimes written *divrei haTorah hazos* (these words of the Torah) and sometimes *sefer haTorah hazeh* (this book of the Torah). (The interpretation of Rashi on this is already known: when *hazos* is used, it refers to the Torah, and when *hazeh* is used, it refers to the word *sefer* or *davar*. However, this itself requires explanation: why is the word sometimes linked in a way that it is written in the feminine, and sometimes in the masculine?) You explain, shlita, that this depends on the manner of Torah study: if the student is as he should be—learning *lishmah* (for its own sake), acting as a *mashpia* (giver)—then it is written as *hazeh*. This was the case in the days of Yehoshua. But if the student is not as he should be, in the capacity of a *mekabel* (receiver), then the term is *hazos*. This was the case in the days of Moshe. This interpretation requires further study, for the *hazeh* and *hazos* in the verse speak of the Torah, not the student.

It appears to me that the explanation is as follows: The literal meaning of learning Torah *lishmah* is "for the sake of the Torah." That is, the Torah is in the capacity of a *mekabel* from the student. This is similar to the various teachings of our Sages that refer to Yisrael as the groom and the Torah as the bride, which is explained at length in *Chassidus*—in *Likutei Torah* (Bracha 94a, Shir HaShirim 39c). Conversely, learning that is not *lishmah*—but is still *dikdusha* (of holiness)—is learning for one's own benefit (as in *Pesachim* 68b; see also *Shaar HaGilgulim*, end of Introduction 4). In that case, the Torah is the *mashpia*, in the capacity of a groom, and Yisrael is in the capacity of a bride. Therefore, in the desert, where they were a "generation of knowledge" and ate the *manna*, their learning was closer to being *lishmah*, and not for the sake of receiving a reward. This is not the case when they came to the Land and their livelihood was through *derech eretz*—a man plowing, etc.—which is contingent upon the condition, "If you walk in My statutes," and as the *Sifra* explains, that you must be toiling in Torah. Consequently, there was room for learning that was not *lishmah*. Therefore, regarding Yehoshua, it is written *hazeh*, because then the Torah is the *mashpia*, and regarding Moshe, it is written *hazos*, because then the Torah is the *mekabel*.

2) At the beginning of the *Chiddushim* on the *Shas* (Part II, p. 253), regarding Chizkiyahu, who was punished for not engaging in *peru u'rvu* (procreation), and whose prayer was, "I have done what is good in Your eyes," as he joined the redemption to the prayer.

According to what is explained in *Chassidus* and *Kabbalah*, one can explain the connection between these two matters—his sin and his prayer—as well as the connection between "I have done what is good in Your eyes" and joining the redemption to the prayer. For *Geulah* (redemption) is the *sefirah* of *Yesod*, and *Tefillah* (prayer) is the *sefirah* of *Malchus*. Similarly, "good" is the attribute of *Yesod*, and "in Your eyes I have done" refers to *Malchus*. The joining of *Geulah* to *Tefillah* and their unification is the matter of the "higher unification," the *Tefillah Arichta* (extended prayer), through which, subsequently, in the *Shemoneh Esrei*—and especially in the *Sim Shalom* blessing—there is a unification of *ZON* (Ze'er Anpin and Nukvah) and the fulfillment of the request in practice and in the physical realm. This was Chizkiyahu’s justification: that he engaged in *peru u'rvu* in a spiritual sense, and in such a way that it was also drawn down into the physical (which is the advantage and superiority of *Tefillah* over *Torah*, as explained in *Iggeret HaKodesh* by our Rebbe, the author of the *Tanya*, *Kuntres Acharon*, p. 158a). These are the words of the *Tzemach Tzedek* in his book *Or HaTorah* (s.d. *HaMalach HaGoel*): "'And I have done what is good in Your eyes'—he connected the attribute of 'good' to *Malchus*, which is called 'eye'." He cites there the *Zohar* (Vol. III, 128b) and the *Matok MiDvash* there.

*Shaarei Orah*, *Shaar Bet* (and not like the *Zoharei Chamma*, beginning of *Parshas Vayigash*). See also the writings of the *Arizal*, and in *Pri Etz Chaim* before *Shemoneh Esrei* and in the *Sim Shalom* blessing.

3) In the *Chiddushim* on the holy *Zohar*, page 292: Regarding what is stated in *Zohar Yisro* and *Kedoshim* (Vol. II, 89a; Vol. III, 81a), "Remember the day of Shabbos to sanctify it," and it is written, "You shall be holy," etc.—it does not say "they were," but "you shall be," which is certain.

Seemingly, the explanation is that the difference between the holiness of Shabbos and *Yom Tov* is that Shabbos is sanctified and established, and is not dependent on the lower beings at all, whereas regarding *Yom Tov*, the lower beings sanctify the times. And yet, the order of Shabbos is that it counts six days first and then sanctifies the seventh (Shabbos 69b; *Shulchan Aruch Orach Chaim*, sec. 344).

Similarly, this is the holiness of Yisrael: "You shall be holy" is certain, for ultimately, "none shall be cast away from Him," and it is impossible to replace them with another nation. But it is written "you shall be" (*tihyu*), because it counts the six days first—the matters of the six thousand years, of which two thousand are *tohu*, two thousand are Torah, and two thousand are the days of *Mashiach*. Finally, these two thousand are the addition of the profane to the holy, the seventh day, the holy Shabbos, the seventh millennium, as per the *Ramban* on the Torah (*Bereishis* 2:3). And then, "you shall be holy."

4) Regarding the note in *Zohar Behar* (p. 108a) that "slaves are exempt from the yoke of the Kingdom of Heaven."

Surely it has not escaped you, shlita, that the commentators on the *Zohar* have addressed this, citing *Shaar Chadash*, *Sefer HaItur*, *Hilchos Tefillin*, which explains this *Zohar* to exclude a Hebrew slave from *Tefillin*. They also cite *Maseches Kiddushin* (22b), the *Yerushalmi* there (Chapter 1, end of Halacha 2), *Yerushalmi Berachos* (Chapter 3, Halacha 3), the book *Zecher Yehosef* on *Chagigah* (p. 4, end of 1a), the book *Yekar Tiferes* by the *Radbaz* on *Hilchos Avadim* (Chapter 1, Halacha 1), and others.

5) At the end of the book, the explanation of the *Midrash* in *Koheles Rabbah* (8:5) on the verse, "He who keeps a commandment... a wise heart."

In the *Yalkut Shimoni* (see also *Koheles Zuta*), the *Midrash* is found at greater length (the words of Torah are poor in one place and rich in another). It interprets the verse regarding Aharon and Moshe, Yehoshua and Moshe, Esther and Mordechai.

—It is also brought there that it refers to Yosef, but that interpretation applies only to the beginning of the verse, whereas the aforementioned interpretation explains the entire verse.—

From this it is evident (especially from the interpretation of Yehoshua and Moshe) that the opinion of our Sages is that the "wise man" of the verse is greater than the "keeper of the commandment."

Briefly, one could explain that Moshe and Aharon are the "best man of the King" and the "best man of the Queen." Moshe and Yehoshua are the "face of the sun" and the "face of the moon," the *mashpia* and the *mekabel*. Likewise, Mordechai and Esther. It is understood that Above, the knowledge of evil does not cause harm, whereas man, from his "lower" side, by knowing good and evil, causes sin and death (explained at length in *Torah Or*—by the author of the *Tanya*—s.d. *VeHanachash Hayah Arum*). Therefore, for Moshe, the "best man of the King," it did not cause harm, as he knew that "I will be sanctified through those who are near to Me" (as cited there), whereas for the "best man of the Queen" and his children, it was necessary to conceal this.

Similarly, this is the matter of the incident of the Golden Calf regarding Moshe and Yehoshua, and Haman's decree regarding Mordechai and Esther. The proof is that when they told Esther, she had her period—"impure blood" and a source of nourishment for the *chitzonim* (external forces). This is similar to one whose mind is strong: bad news regarding the future, G-d forbid, does not distract him from his affairs, unlike women, whose minds are "light." Esther was protected from this knowledge, and there was no room for the nourishment of the *chitzonim* because she was occupied with the *biur* (eradication) of *chametz* specifically—that is, the total nullification of evil. This is similar to the words of our Sages (*Pesachim* 11a): "He himself searches for it to burn it; he does not eat from it."

May the Almighty protect everyone among the entirety of Yisrael, and redeem us from being slaves to Achashverosh, that we may be holy in truth, and do what is good in His eyes with joy, inwardly and completely.

## 2515

B"H, 17 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

...Since your work involves holy tasks, serving in the shul, you have surely heard the saying that my father-in-law, the Rebbe, related in the name of the Tzemach Tzedek, who once told someone: "Call the *shamash* (sexton) and light will shine" (*kera lashemesh veyizrach or*). The role of a *shamash* is to ensure that the shul is illuminated—by keeping the shul open for more hours during the day, and by establishing *shiurei Torah* in public and the recitation of Tehillim *betzibur*.

May Hashem help you to carry this out without any obstacles, and may this serve as a *tzinor* (conduit) for blessing and success in your personal affairs.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe,

## 219

B"H, 21 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

I received word from the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, and distinguished individual, who is involved in communal needs, etc., Moreinu Harav [Name], that your health, may you be well, is not as it should be, and he requested that I mention you for a recovery. When I am at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, I will mention you for a complete and speedy recovery. May Hashem grant each of us the merit to bring each other only good tidings.

I will take this opportunity—relying on the Sages' teaching that "one only urges those who are already diligent"—to encourage you, may you be well, regarding a matter that is well-known and visibly apparent: the necessity for every yeshiva and educational institution to exert themselves more and more in instilling matters of fear of Heaven in their students. This should be done not only by supervising their conduct, but also through appropriate studies. Since it is the nature of a yeshiva student to seek an explanation for everything—not merely to follow a command as a simple order—it is necessary to teach them the appropriate subject matter for this. This has led to a consensus in all the yeshivos to institute the study of Mussar or the study of Chassidus.

There is a difference between the study of Mussar and the study of Chassidus regarding this: generally, the study of Mussar humbles and consequently constricts the learner, whereas the study of Chassidus elevates a person and brings about an expansion within him (even though, of course, there are aspects of Mussar study that bring joy and expansion, and aspects of Chassidus that lead to submission and constriction). Furthermore, the study of Chassidus is more likely to lead to the love and fear of Hashem, in accordance with what is explained in the Rambam, Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah, regarding how one arrives at His love and fear—by contemplating, etc.

Since you, may you be well, have been granted the merit from Heaven to be appointed as a Rosh Yeshiva and to have influence over many avreichim and bochurim, you have certainly been given the strength for this as well, in accordance with the demands and the necessity of the hour, to influence those under your guidance to draw closer to the study of Chassidus.

Surely, for someone such as yourself, no explanation is needed that while several generations ago it was possible for a yeshiva to suffice without any study of Mussar—since even the "street" was G-d-fearing—the situation changed in the two generations prior to this one, as undesirable winds began to blow through the world and started to penetrate the walls of the yeshiva. Consequently, the study of Mussar was introduced into the yeshiva curriculum. Now, our current generation has changed; it is a double and redoubled darkness, where it is impossible to fulfill one's obligation with the study of Mussar alone. It is a necessity that every ben Torah also study the inner dimension of the Torah—referred to in the holy Zohar as the "soul of the Torah"—in order to thereby safeguard the study of the revealed Torah and to enable the youth of this generation to arrive at the love and fear of Hashem, as mentioned above. It is already well-known that the Arizal ruled that it is specifically in this generation that it is a mitzvah to reveal this wisdom (referring to the inner dimension of the Torah).

With respect and blessings for a speedy recovery.

## 2020

2020

B”H, 21 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter from 8 Shevat, to which I have been delayed in responding due to a multitude of preoccupations.

While on the one hand, I was pleased to read that our conversation stirred a doubt in your heart as to whether it might be worthwhile to adopt a path of conduct consistent with the tradition of our ancestors, on the other hand, I am pained that this doubt remains merely in your contemplation and has not yet found expression in your day-to-day life. For ultimately, this is the main thing; every day and every moment that does not conform to this conduct is a loss that can never be recovered.

As I mentioned to you briefly during our conversation, if you weigh the reward against the loss—and the inverse—involved in both approaches, you will see that they are not comparable at all. If a person conducts himself according to the path of Torah and mitzvos on the assumption that, over time, this will lead to a firm decision that this is indeed how one should live, he acquires his world and lives his years in a manner consistent with the truth. Even if he were to remain in doubt for all his days for some reason or another (which, according to our Torah, is akin to an illness), or even if he were to eventually reach the opposite conclusion, the loss would merely be that he spent a portion of his time on matters that did not yield him benefit.

However, on the other hand, if you wait to act in practice until you reach a firm decision—which, in most cases, takes a great deal of time, as the very lack of action adds difficulties in understanding the matter correctly—then if you do eventually arrive at the correct decision, you will find that you have lost all the years that have passed. Not only have they failed to bring the possible benefits, but you have also squandered your life in a way that has harmed yourself, and consequently, the entire world, of which you are a part. Regarding such a situation, our Sages, of blessed memory, said: "Calculate the loss of a mitzvah against its reward, and the reward of a transgression against its loss."

Regarding your personal matter and the practical problem you wrote about, as is customary, I have referred the entire matter to the secretariat, and they will be in direct contact with you.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 2223

2223

B"H, 22 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

I received your letter of 17 Teves, in which you outline your life and your study regimen until now.

It is surprising that you do not mention whether you have a set time for the study of Chassidus or Mussar. If such study was essential at all times, it is all the more so in our current era—the generation of the *ikvesa d’meshicha*—where the concealments and obscurations regarding matters of *yiras shamayim* and the actual performance of *mitzvos* are so great. This is especially true when one is located in a place where there are not many *chareidi* Jews. Part of these concealments and obscurations also includes the various questions people raise regarding the matters of Divine Providence. The intent behind these questions is not to resolve them, but rather to find a pretext to be lenient in the observance of *mitzvos*; sometimes, these questions are even used by the *yetzer hara* to justify casting off the yoke of the Kingdom of Heaven.

It is understood that it is difficult to explain at length in a letter all these questions, which are nothing more than the counsel of the *yetzer*. Especially since, as mentioned, most of those who raise these questions have no intention of accepting an answer, but rather seek a justification to walk in a crooked path. Nevertheless, I will attempt to briefly address what was asked of you: how is it possible to believe in *hashgacha pratis*, or in general that Hashem is the Master of the world and its Guide, at a time when we see, for example, that last year in Holland the sea flooded part of the land and, G-d forbid, several hundred men, women, and children were lost, in addition to the damage to property and assets?

The explanation of this matter can be understood through an analogy from a different field. Imagine a person who has never lived in a civilized society and was raised alone in the desert. Suddenly, he is brought into the office of a dentist or a surgeon. He sees a child sitting in a chair with his hands and feet tied, and the dentist is extracting one of his teeth. The child is screaming at the top of his lungs. Or, in the case of the surgeon, he sees a person tied to a table in such a way that he cannot move his limbs to protect himself or escape, and they are operating on him and shedding his blood. It is self-evident that someone who has no knowledge of whether these doctors are honest and righteous people—and certainly no knowledge of the science of medicine—will reach the absolute conclusion that these people handling the child or the patient are nothing but bandits whose only intent is to cause great and immense pain to those who fall into their hands.

However, if this visitor from the desert had knowledge of the methods of medicine and the human body—that sometimes it is necessary to perform surgery or extract a decayed tooth—even though the pain is extreme at the moment, he would know that this will save the person from prolonged and agonizing suffering and that his body will be healthy for decades to come. And even if this person has no knowledge of the science of medicine, but he is certain of the righteousness and goodness of the dentist or the surgeon, then although he would be pained by the suffering of the child or the patient, it would be clear to him that the momentary pain is worth it for the sake of the benefit that will last for decades.

The same applies in our case. Each of us sees temporary events that unfold over the course of a few days, months, or years, and we completely forget that the *neshamah* existed for thousands of years beforehand. Since the *neshamah* is eternal, it will continue to exist forever after this as well. If, through the suffering of a brief moment—brief relative to the time of the *neshamah’s* life before and after this event—an eternal benefit is achieved for all the time past and future, then not only would one not complain about the suffering, but, in the words of the prophet (Yeshayahu 12:1), he would say, "I thank You, Hashem, for You were angry with me."

It is understood that there is still room for another question: why is the order of creation such that in order to merit goodness, one must pass through matters of "not-good"—like the extraction of a tooth or the surgery in the examples above? Or, more broadly, why does Hashem not give everyone all their needs as a free gift, without any effort on the part of the recipient? On the contrary, our Sages said, "According to the pain is the reward." Since Hashem is the essence of Good, seemingly He should bestow all needs without the recipient having to do anything for it? This matter, too, is explained at length in our holy books, and this is not the place for it.

May the *Ribono shel Olam* grant you success in your Torah study with pure *yiras shamayim*, without becoming entangled in *pilpulim* and questions. Believe in *Havayah* with a complete and simple faith that He is the essence of Good. Consequently, one can rely absolutely that all His ways are good and upright, and "the Rock, His work is perfect." Walk your path securely—the path of Torah and *mitzvos*—and delve deeper into the *avodah* of the Creator in this direction, with joy, by setting fixed times to study the teachings of Chassidus.

Regarding the support of your mother, may she live and be well, I have instructed my office to look into whether it is possible to do something about this, and they will write to you.

With blessings.

## 2525

B"H, 23 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

I have just received your letter of 18 Shevat, and I am hastening to reply, for I was alarmed by the conclusion of your letter that due to the state of your health, "it is very possible that I will resign from my position in the coming days." Although my hope is strong that this is merely a fleeting thought—a *ka-salka-da'atach* and a *hava-amina*—even this is cause for regret, for even a *ka-salka-da'atach* leaves an impression on the energy of the one who harbors it, and even on his surroundings.

Surely for one such as yourself, who is on-site in Eretz Yisrael (may it be built and established), there is no need for length or explanation regarding the necessity for every single person in the camp of the *chareidim* not only to stand at his post in public life, but to increase his courage in this, for "the measure of good is greater than the measure of..." And if the opposing side is mobilizing all its forces, then how much more so must this be the case in the camp of the *chareidim*.

Regarding what you write concerning the state of your health, it is well known the saying of our Rebbe, the Alter Rebbe, author of the Tanya and Shulchan Aruch, regarding the preciousness and importance of the body of a Jewish man. The Rambam has already ruled (*Hilchos De'os* 4:1) that having a healthy and sound body is among the ways of Hashem. However, you must certainly arrange your *avodah* in such a way that it does not strain your health; then, on the contrary, the *avodah* serves as a channel and vessel for strengthening your health. Beyond what is understood simply and according to the law of our Torah regarding the "populace of Mechoza" (*Bava Metzia* 77a; see also Rosh there), it is simple in terms of spirituality that actions taken to raise the banner of *Yisrael Sava*—especially when you labor in this—draw down the provision of "your rains in their time," and as is explained in the books of Poland, "your rains" (*gishmeichem*) is related to the word *gashmius* (materiality).

With respect and blessing,

Waiting for good news.

## 2296

B"H, 23 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi, the pious and G-d-fearing, a descendant of holy lineage, etc.,
Rabbi Yechiel, may he live and be well,

Greetings and blessings!

It was with pleasure that I received your letter bearing the good news that you have betrothed your daughter, Mrs. Chava Yehudis, may she live, and have set the wedding date for the first day of Rosh Chodesh Adar Rishon, may it come upon us for good.

May it be the will of Hashem that it be for good fortune, for good fortune, and at a favorable and successful time, and that they build an everlasting edifice based on the foundations of Torah and mitzvos, as they are illuminated by the "light of Torah"—which is the teachings of Chassidus. May you and your wife, the Rabbanit, may they live, derive from them an abundance of Chassidishe nachas.

With respect and with blessings for good fortune, good fortune.

It is known from the words of our Sages that "when Adar enters, we increase in joy" (cited as halacha in Magen Avraham, Orach Chaim 686:6; see also Shu"t Chasam Sofer, Orach Chaim, siman 160, and Nimukei Orach Chaim there—by the author of Minchas Ani). And according to what is written in the Rema, Orach Chaim 668:7, this applies from Adar Rishon. And thus, according to the responsa of the Geonim and the Mahariitz Giat (cited in Tur, Orach Chaim 620).

And see in the sefer [which is] cited in Sdei Chemed, Pe'as HaSadeh, Klalim, subsection 82, regarding the presumption of joy occurring three times: Adar, Rosh Chodesh, and the joy of a wedding. And a presumption is established for the future.

## 2,227

B"H, 23 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the esteemed Rabbi and Chassid, G-d-fearing and distinguished, engaged in the needs of the community, etc.,
Rabbi Binyamin Eliyahu Gorodetzky, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

... I have now received the report of the 5712 annual meeting of the Rabbinical Council in Morocco. I also saw there a brief overview regarding the state of education in the large towns, and I was alarmed by the lack of representation of the work of Oholei Yosef Yitzchok. It seems this is only due to a lack of effort in publicizing it, for in several places in the report, it is evident that there is a positive attitude toward Lubavitch. And while, on the other hand, it is perhaps not advisable to over-publicize the work of Lubavitch, so that the *eyna bisha* (evil eye) and jealousy do not take hold, to pass over it in such great silence is the other extreme, which is also, of course, undesirable. Apparently, based on their practice, they will also print a report for the year 5713, and surely there is still time to correct the aforementioned there, at least in part.

I also saw in the aforementioned report that they mention Fez, Casablanca, Mogador, and Port Lyautey as the primary towns. It seems that no effort was made from Lubavitch to become informed about the state of education there, and perhaps it is worth making an effort in this regard.

With blessings for success, awaiting good news regarding the above.

#### ***2,227***

**Rabbi Binyamin Eliyahu Gorodetzky, may he live:** Additional letters to him appear above in Vol. 7, 2,228, and in the references noted in the footnotes there.

**Dar-el-Beida:** This is Casablanca.

## 2228

2228

B.H., 23 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn

To Anash and the Temimim in Montreal,
May Hashem keep them alive.

Greetings and blessings!

It was a pleasure to receive regards from you, initially through the esteemed and G-d-fearing chossid, who toils for the needs of the community, Rabbi Ben Zion Shemtov, who, at my request, visited the homes of several of you. Subsequently, I received regards through those traveling from there to here for the Yahrtzeit of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, the leader of our generation.

There is joy on one hand regarding the general report that, thank G-d, your numbers have increased—may they continue to increase. Yet, there is sorrow on the other hand regarding the situation concerning quality. Even if the situation had remained as it was before, that is not the ultimate goal and intent, for the ruling of the Torah is that one must always "ascend in matters of holiness." This is especially true according to our Sages, who state that anyone in whose days the Beis Hamikdash is not rebuilt is as if it were destroyed [by them]. This is further elucidated by my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, with a parable: one might remain in their current standing and position while walking on level ground, but when climbing a very steep mountain, there are only two options: either to climb and ascend upward, or, Heaven forbid, to descend downward.

Regarding the institution that has long been called a Yeshiva: despite all the urging and effort, it remains in the state of a Talmud Torah (though in America, they have adorned it with the title "Yeshiva Ketana"). Even though several avreichim were compelled to remain there, this has apparently not at all stirred the administration to address the situation and make the institution fit the name that our Nasi gave it—namely, a Yeshiva. It is also called Yeshivas Tomchei Temimim, and the concept of a "Tamim" has already been explained in many sichos and maamarim. The study, and primarily the supervision, must be conducted in a manner appropriate to that title. You can see for yourselves how far the reality is, not only from the ideal, but even from what is proper.

Since "He looked into the Torah and created the world"—both the macrocosm and the microcosm, which is man—any rectification concerning a person must also be connected to matters of Torah. Therefore, you should certainly increase your study sessions in Nigleh and Chassidus, both in public and in private, as well as in matters of farbrengens. You have with you the esteemed and G-d-fearing chossid, one of the elders of Anash, Rabbi Peretz Motchkin, who can dedicate most of his time to these matters. You will alleviate his burdens regarding physical livelihood, and he will alleviate (or at least strive to alleviate) your burdens regarding spiritual livelihood.

May it be the will of Hashem that, in accordance with what was discussed during the farbrengen on the fourth day of the Yahrtzeit—that although the first three years were "uncircumcised" and the fourth year was only for matters of holiness in potential—in the fifth year, there will be an increase in your camp and within the "four cubits" of each of you: your produce, both the produce of the field and the produce of the vineyard, as their concepts are explained in many places in Chassidus. And it will be as the end of the verse: "I am Hashem your G-d." Rashi explains: "I am Hashem"—who promises this and is faithful to keep My promise; "your G-d"—your strength and your vitality, in all of this.

Awaiting good news soon, and blessing you with extraordinary success in all of the above, both in general matters and in your personal affairs, for one is dependent upon the other.

B"H, 23 Shevat, 5714

**M. Schneerson**

P.S.

Regarding the manner and frequency of study with the young children and their meals, as well as the arrangement of separate classes for boys and girls—there are two points that must be kept in mind:

a) That the local residents should not have grounds to say, "We have come from a higher level of holiness to a lower one" (and in any case, the aforementioned separation, from a certain age—in accordance with the nature of the local people—is necessary).

b) Do not overstretch the string—and in accordance with the above, you should arrange these matters. It would be best if there were a uniform method of conduct in this regard for all the Ohalei Yosef Yitzchak institutions in Morocco.

## 240

B.H., 24 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

After the long hiatus, I was pleased to receive your letter of 17 Shevat, in which you write about your livelihood situation, as well as your *shiurim*, and conclude by mentioning that the workers at the factory where you are employed are not *frum*.

Regarding this, rely on what is explained at length in *Tanya*: that every single Jew possesses a hidden love—which includes *yirah*—and it is an inheritance bequeathed to us by our forefathers. If so, they lack nothing but an awakener to reveal the inner point within them. Through the *ruach chaim* (spirit of life), this spark can become a great torch that will illuminate their bodies and *nefesh habehamis*, and subsequently their homes and surroundings as well. Since your material livelihood comes—at least in its greater part—from this factory, it is reasonable to say that your spiritual livelihood is also rooted there, and it is achieved through the aforementioned [process]. When one acts with the appropriate vigor, one succeeds...

Certainly, in addition to the *shiurim* you mention in your letter, you also have a *shiur* in the study of *halachos* that pertain to daily life, most of which are found in the section of *Orach Chaim*. May Hashem grant you success in all of the above.

With blessings for livelihood in abundance.

## 2331

2331

B"H, 24 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 20 Shevat, in which you mention that you are being offered work to counter the mission [missionary activity], G-d forbid.

You do not provide details regarding the nature of the work; in any case, the work of saving souls does not require the consent of anyone. The methods of work must, of course, be such that they bring the greatest benefit. May Hashem grant you success in this and in all your affairs.

With blessings for success in your work in the vineyard of Chabad.

I am **very** surprised regarding the changing of the knot of the tefillin from Chabad to Polish custom. After all, everyone agrees that the Maggid, who succeeded the Baal Shem Tov, specifically entrusted our Alter Rebbe with the task of compiling the Shulchan Aruch, and throughout the entire land of Poland he is referred to simply as **"the Rav."** If there are certain instructions of the Alter Rebbe that were not accepted due to various reasons, that is one thing; but to accept one of his halachic rulings in practice and then to retract it—this is a puzzling and unfounded matter.

May Hashem guide you in paths of righteousness and truth.

## 2332

B"H, 24 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Professor Yechezkel Kutscher, may he live and be well,

Shalom and blessings!

It was a pleasure to meet you and to discuss with you some of the issues currently at the forefront of the world of Israeli youth in particular. In accordance with the saying of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, that when two Jews meet, beyond the benefit that accrues to both of them, the meeting should also bring benefit to others, I hope that our meeting, too, will bring benefit to those they influence and to the surrounding environment.

This is especially true regarding the youth, for "man is a tree of the field." Any slight change in a tender sapling—and all the more so in the seed—can influence the entire course of the tree's life, for better or, G-d forbid, for worse. Especially in these days, when destructive winds blow not only in the street, but have also penetrated educational institutions and even yeshivos, the responsibility (and consequently, the merit) of all those who have the capacity to influence and whose voices are heard among the youth is many times greater.

These educators and influential figures must view themselves as the shluchim of the Ribono shel Olam in a specific place and at a specific time, to illuminate, clarify, refine, and sanctify their environment with the "candle of a mitzvah and the light of Torah," and the "light within the Torah."

For the power of this light is sufficient to bring back to the right path even those who have drifted to the very furthest extreme, as our Sages of blessed memory stated (Eichah Rabbah, beginning of Petichta 2): "There is nothing that can stand before the will."

With respect and with blessings for success in fulfilling this holy shlichus with peace of mind.

Thank you for sending me your article, "The Linguistic Background of the Isaiah Scroll," for me to read; it is enclosed herewith as you requested.

By the way, regarding your explanation at the end of your article concerning the change of words in the Torah of Rabbi Meir—that it was a popular manuscript or influenced by such a manuscript, etc.—in my opinion, there is no room for this explanation. Rabbi Meir was accepted as the "chief of the scribes" in his day, as the story of our Sages of blessed memory relates that he would write from memory and then copy from that with the greatest possible precision (Megillah 18b). This is also necessitated by one of the changes in the Torah of Rabbi Meir, where it is written "tov mot" (death is good) instead of "tov me'od" (very good) (Bereishis Rabbah 9:5); this, on the contrary, testifies to a deep concept and interpretation, not to a popular version. This is especially true given that the greatest scholars of the generation praised him in the field of scribal arts (Eiruvin 13a; Gittin 67a; Koheles Rabbah 2:17).

#### ***2332***

**Note:** See also letter 2592 below.

## 2733

2733

B”H, 24 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 21 Shevat, in which you write regarding the matter of naming your grandchild, which was done by your daughter’s mother-in-law, may they live.

According to what is written in the seforim, the naming of a newborn child should be done by the father and mother.

It is self-understood that they may authorize someone else to perform this in their name. If in this instance that was not done, you should see to it that your daughter and your son-in-law speak with one another and reach a mutual agreement as to what name to give the child.

And since a *Mi Sheberach* has already been made and the child already has a name, one should not abandon that name, *chas v'shalom*. However, one may add another name. May Hashem help you with Yiddishe nachas from all your family, with *harchavas hada'as*.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

## 2,334

B"H, 25 Shevat, 5714
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

The fact that your wife will take upon herself to wear a sheitel with a joyful heart, and will not be ashamed before those who might—perhaps—mock her for it, will be to her great merit, especially as she will be among the first in her environment to bring back this custom of the kosher daughters of Yisrael. It is already well-known how our Sages of blessed memory have extolled the virtue of being one who brings merit to the public.

It may be that, temporarily, this is difficult to do due to the expense involved. There is a special fund available that provides loans for such purposes, to be paid back over a longer period, in order to make this possible for everyone. Therefore, it is not worthwhile to delay this. As soon as you receive this letter, write to me regarding the name to whom the check should be made out and the amount, and it will be sent out immediately. May Hashem Yisbarach grant you success.
